Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Arafat wuquf ♥ The key ritual within the wider sequence of rituals that makes up the Hajj. “On the morning of the ninth, [pilgrims] proceed towards ‘Arafat, a plain 14.5km (nine miles) from Mina, where the central rite of the Hajj, wuquf (standing) on ‘Arafat, takes place between noon and sunset, during which time many heartfelt prayers are offered. The scene reminds Muslims of the gathering on the Day of Judgement. If any pilgrim misses this event, his/her Hajj is not valid and has to be done again another year. This does not apply to any other rite, all of which can be done over a longer period or compensated for with an offering. This is the time for the pilgrims to read the Qur’an, glorify God and pray most earnestly for forgiveness and everything else they wish for. Particularly recommended for glorification and repetition is this prayer, ‘There is no God but Allah alone, with no partner. Dominion and praise belong to Him, who gives life and death. Goodness is in His hands and He has power over everything.’ This just gives a succinct summary of the Islamic faith and connects the individual pilgrim to what Ibrahim and Muhammad brought and uttered. Pilgrims normally face towards the Ka‘ba while reciting these prayers, as they do for the five daily prayers, believing that, by doing this, their prayers are more likely to be accepted.” [545]. This ritual is coded here as dysphoric due to the fact that pilgrims are required to stand for several hours (between noon and sunset) under the sun’s heat. A contemporary account, dating to 1997, notes that the “heat is virtually unbearable” [546]. However, it is worth noting that, due to peculiarities of the Islamic calendar, this ritual may sometimes be performed at a time of the year when the temperature is less hot than at other times, and when there are fewer others between noon and sunset than at other times. Moreover, a few informal online sources suggest that those who cannot stand in the sun for the duration of the ritual are allowed to be in the shade and/or sit down. Still, though relatively mild, this appears to be the most dysphoric ritual within Sunni Islam.
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ The main acts of the Hajj take place in the twelfth month of the Islamic calendar [547].
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ once in a lifetime ♥ Every Muslim should undertake the Hajj pilgrimage at least once in their lifetime.[548]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Duration ♣ 6 ♥ hours, on average. The ritual takes place between noon and sunset.
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ substantial minority ♥ Religious specialists would have been Muslims, and the Caliphate's rulers were Muslims, which means that religious specialists came from the dominant religious group.
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ substantial minority ♥ all Muslims[549]
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ suspected unknown ♥
Dysphoric elements
♠ fasting ♣ inferred present ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [550], but, because participants must devote all their energies in prayer, it seems likely that they were not allowed to eat. Then again, this may not count as fasting due to the relative brevity of the ritual (between 5 and 7 hours, depending on the time of the year when it is performed on any given year).
♠ vigil/sleep deprivation ♣ absent ♥ Though the ritual is a vigil, it takes place during the daytime [551].
♠ fear ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [552].
♠ humiliation ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [553].
♠ disgust ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [554].
♠ poisoning/dysphoria-causing drugs ♣ absent ♥ Islam prohibits the consumption of mind-altering substances.[555]
♠ pain ♣ inferred absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [556].
♠ mutilation ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [557].
♠ risk of death ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [558].
♠ other ♣ present ♥ Ideally, participants would stand under the sun for several hours between noon and sunset [559][560].
Euphoric elements
♠ feasting ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [561].
♠ alcohol ♣ absent ♥ Islam prohibits the consumption of mind-altering substances. [562]
♠ euphoria-inducing drugs (other than alcohol) ♣ absent ♥ Islam prohibits the consumption of mind-altering substances. [563]
♠ sex ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [564].
♠ dancing ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [565].

♠ honour code ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [569].
♠ oath taking ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [570].

Costs of participation
♠ currency ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [574].
♠ property/valuable items ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [575].
♠ health costs ♣ inferred absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [576]. It appears that those who are too weak to stand under the sun are allowed to sit and/or be in the shade, though a serious academic source has not been found to confirm this.
♠ animal sacrifice ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual [577].
♠ human sacrifice of an out-group member ♣ absent ♥ [Text from sacrifice codings by Agathe] No source could be found that explicitly states Islam’s condemnation of human sacrifice. However, the fact that no source could be found that even ‘’mentions’’ human sacrifice in connection with Islam (beyond the occasional lurid, and dubious, tabloid headline), combined with the fact that a lot of information is available on the beliefs and practices underlining this religion, strongly suggests that human sacrifice is not part of the Islamic ritual repertoire. ““Our” present religions, Judaism, Christianity, and Islam, sometimes called Abrahamic religions, have the theme of human sacrifice right at their inception, with the founding father, Abraham. We are accustomed to the story, which remains a text of “terror and trembling,” as Kierkegaard put it. Abraham is commanded by god to “sacrifice” his only son Isaac, and he is willing to do so (Gen. 22). The Hebrew term for this sacrifice is colah, “make to rise upwards,” as fire and smoke rise to heaven;10 the action is to cut the throat with a knife and to burn the corpse on wood—the knife and wood expressly mentioned in the text. The human victim must be fettered in order to receive the deadly cut. Abraham does obey his god’s command, without even an attempt at protest. The text enjoys the paradox: “the son that you love”; “and the two walked together” (Gen. 22.1-19: 2;. In Muslim tradition, the place where Abraham “bound” his son is the rock on the temple hill of Jerusalem, which, since Khalif Omar, is covered by the “temple dome” with its golden cupola. Of course, an angel stops Abraham’s action at the last moment, pointing to an animal for sacrifice. The text says God was “testing” (nissah) Abraham, and his submissive obedience is highly rewarded. The text also states that god accepted the ram “instead of” (tachath) Isaac. One way to discard the terror of the story is to introduce a perspective of historical progress: God was replacing human sacrifice by animal sacrifice. This could be termed an advance in civilization. The Islamic interpretation comes close to this interpretation, as it makes this a founding myth for el eid.”

[578]

♠ human sacrifice of an in-group member ♣ absent ♥ [Text from sacrifice codings by Agathe] No source could be found that explicitly states Islam’s condemnation of human sacrifice. However, the fact that no source could be found that even ‘’mentions’’ human sacrifice in connection with Islam (beyond the occasional lurid, and dubious, tabloid headline), combined with the fact that a lot of information is available on the beliefs and practices underlining this religion, strongly suggests that human sacrifice is not part of the Islamic ritual repertoire. ““Our” present religions, Judaism, Christianity, and Islam, sometimes called Abrahamic religions, have the theme of human sacrifice right at their inception, with the founding father, Abraham. We are accustomed to the story, which remains a text of “terror and trembling,” as Kierkegaard put it. Abraham is commanded by god to “sacrifice” his only son Isaac, and he is willing to do so (Gen. 22). The Hebrew term for this sacrifice is colah, “make to rise upwards,” as fire and smoke rise to heaven;10 the action is to cut the throat with a knife and to burn the corpse on wood—the knife and wood expressly mentioned in the text. The human victim must be fettered in order to receive the deadly cut. Abraham does obey his god’s command, without even an attempt at protest. The text enjoys the paradox: “the son that you love”; “and the two walked together” (Gen. 22.1-19: 2;. In Muslim tradition, the place where Abraham “bound” his son is the rock on the temple hill of Jerusalem, which, since Khalif Omar, is covered by the “temple dome” with its golden cupola. Of course, an angel stops Abraham’s action at the last moment, pointing to an animal for sacrifice. The text says God was “testing” (nissah) Abraham, and his submissive obedience is highly rewarded. The text also states that god accepted the ram “instead of” (tachath) Isaac. One way to discard the terror of the story is to introduce a perspective of historical progress: God was replacing human sacrifice by animal sacrifice. This could be termed an advance in civilization. The Islamic interpretation comes close to this interpretation, as it makes this a founding myth for el eid.”

[579]

♠ human sacrifice of a relative ♣ absent ♥ [Text from sacrifice codings by Agathe] No source could be found that explicitly states Islam’s condemnation of human sacrifice. However, the fact that no source could be found that even ‘’mentions’’ human sacrifice in connection with Islam (beyond the occasional lurid, and dubious, tabloid headline), combined with the fact that a lot of information is available on the beliefs and practices underlining this religion, strongly suggests that human sacrifice is not part of the Islamic ritual repertoire. ““Our” present religions, Judaism, Christianity, and Islam, sometimes called Abrahamic religions, have the theme of human sacrifice right at their inception, with the founding father, Abraham. We are accustomed to the story, which remains a text of “terror and trembling,” as Kierkegaard put it. Abraham is commanded by god to “sacrifice” his only son Isaac, and he is willing to do so (Gen. 22). The Hebrew term for this sacrifice is colah, “make to rise upwards,” as fire and smoke rise to heaven;10 the action is to cut the throat with a knife and to burn the corpse on wood—the knife and wood expressly mentioned in the text. The human victim must be fettered in order to receive the deadly cut. Abraham does obey his god’s command, without even an attempt at protest. The text enjoys the paradox: “the son that you love”; “and the two walked together” (Gen. 22.1-19: 2;. In Muslim tradition, the place where Abraham “bound” his son is the rock on the temple hill of Jerusalem, which, since Khalif Omar, is covered by the “temple dome” with its golden cupola. Of course, an angel stops Abraham’s action at the last moment, pointing to an animal for sacrifice. The text says God was “testing” (nissah) Abraham, and his submissive obedience is highly rewarded. The text also states that god accepted the ram “instead of” (tachath) Isaac. One way to discard the terror of the story is to introduce a perspective of historical progress: God was replacing human sacrifice by animal sacrifice. This could be termed an advance in civilization. The Islamic interpretation comes close to this interpretation, as it makes this a founding myth for el eid.”[580]

Phase II Variables (polity-based)[edit]

Institutional Variables[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Edward Turner; Jill Levine ♥ these codes were reviewed at Seshat Workshops, Oxford 2016 and 2017

♠ Expert ♣ ♥

♠ Editor ♣ ♥


Limits on Power of the Chief Executive[edit]

Legal (formal) limits[edit]

♠ Executive power is separate or independent from military ♣ absent: 909-969 CE; present: 970-1035 CE; [present; absent]: 1036-1072 CE; absent: 1073-1171 CE ♥ In Tunisia the imam-caliph controlled the military through his general. Egypt was conquered by al-Mu'izz's general Jawhar who became vizier in Egypt. In Egypt the military authority was delegated to the vizier... "The Faitimid military was a standing professional army."[581] "The Fatimids in North Africa had appointed no wazirs and the notion of delegating that level of authority to a subordinate seemed not to have existed."[582] ... At least from 1073 CE the executive was de facto held by the vizier who operated from his own palace so the vizier will be coded as the executive. The transition probably began in the regency of the imam-caliph al-Mustansir. Badr al-Jamaili was "a virtual dictator" although "his rank remained that of a wazir, and he was always theoretically subservient to the imam-caliph."[583] "In actuality he and those who occupied the same office over the next century were formally titled amir al-juyush, 'Commander of the Armies'."[584] In 1073 CE Badr al-Jamali "transformed the army" and thereafter the Fatimid Caliphate was dominated by "military wazirs".[585] The son and successor to the famous vizier Badr al-Jamali after 1094 CE built the Palace of the Vizerate (Dar al-Wizara) which became "the official residence of the vizirs until the end of the Fatimid caliphate".[586] "In the later Egyptian period, the military under Badr al-Jamali (d. 1094) and his son Afdal (d. 1121) became extremely powerful." [587] "The decline was partially arrested by the administration of Badr al-Jamali and his son, al-Afdal, from 1073 to 1121, in which the military chiefs replaced the caliphs as the effective heads of government."[588] From vizier Badr al-Jamali "Henceforth, with minor exceptions, real power in the Fatimid state remained in the hands of viziers who possessed military bases of power and acted independently, while the caliphs remained the nominal heads of state and as Ismaili imams also functioned as supreme leaders of the ismalil da'wa or religious organization. A distinguishing feature of the Fatimid vizierate during its final century is that several viziers were Christians, notably Armenians."[589] Badr's son al-Afdal was murdered in 1121 CE but he had "exercised the same iron-fisted control over the government as had the father."[590]
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from legislative ♣ absent ♥ In Tunisia the caliph directly ruled without a vizier. The court administration was run by a vizier after the move to Egypt. "The Fatimids in North Africa had appointed no wazirs and the notion of delegating that level of authority to a subordinate seemed not to have existed."[591] The court was highly stratified[592] and according to traveller Nasir-i Khusraw (c1048 CE) employed 30,000 people[593], an exaggerated figure which reflects the fact it was involved in more than managing the imam-caliph's harem and personal life, which was controlled by a "hierarchical corps of eunuchs".[594] The court-based government had heads for military, treasury, chancery (domestic and foreign communications), religious and missionary activities, judiciary and an auditor (from 1011 CE).[595] From the early 11th century a state armoury employed 3,000 craftsmen.[596] "Poets were employed by the state to fill the role of modern-day public relations agents"[597] who were "paid to present truth and lies in a palatable manner."[598] ... At least from 1073 CE the executive became de facto held by the vizier who was soon to operate from his own palace so the vizier will be coded as the executive. The transition probably began in the regency of the imam-caliph al-Mustansir. Badr al-Jamaili was "a virtual dictator" although "his rank remained that of a wazir, and he was always theoretically subservient to the imam-caliph."[599] The son and successor to the famous vizier Badr al-Jamali after 1094 CE built the Palace of the Vizerate (Dar al-Wizara) which became "the official residence of the vizirs until the end of the Fatimid caliphate".[600] "The Armenian wazir, Badr al-Jamali, is certainly one of the most famous of these and was the de facto ruler of the Fatimid state (1074-94) as wazir to the Fatimid caliph, al-Mustansir (r. 1036-94)."[601] From vizier Badr al-Jamali "Henceforth, with minor exceptions, real power in the Fatimid state remained in the hands of viziers who possessed military bases of power and acted independently, while the caliphs remained the nominal heads of state and as Ismaili imams also functioned as supreme leaders of the ismalil da'wa or religious organization. A distinguishing feature of the Fatimid vizierate during its final century is that several viziers were Christians, notably Armenians."[602] Badr al-Jamaili was "a virtual dictator" although "his rank remained that of a wazir, and he was always theoretically subservient to the imam-caliph."[603] Badr's son al-Afdal was murdered in 1121 CE but he had "exercised the same iron-fisted control over the government as had the father."[604] In all periods the executive, whether or not it was effectively in the hands of the caliph or the vizier, also held legislative power. The executive exerted power through the court-government. After the Palace of the Vizerate was built the new complex presumably simply replaced the imam-caliph's palace complex as the seat of the government (or created a command centre for organizing it) but the equivalence of the executive and legislative always remained with the court-government.
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from judiciary ♣ absent: 909-1100 CE; present: 1122-1171 CE ♥ Like the position of vizier was not present in the Tunisian period but was after the move to Egypt, the chief qadi was not present until the Egyptian period.[605] "Under this dynasty these were two of the highest positions: the chief justice, termed the judge of judges (qadi al-qudat), was outranked only by the imam and the wazir. The head da'i, the da'i al-du'at, followed immediately below."[606] (Chief qadi and chief da'i position often held by single individual (for example, if they were an Ismaili).[607]). In the Tunisian period the imam-caliph used his ultimate powers of judicial authority and attempted to impose Ismaili law. Al-Mu'izz (953-975 CE) "was responsible for the promulgation and implementation of a distinctive Isma'ili law, mainly formulated by al-Qadi al-Nu'man, which aimed at imprinting with an Isma'ili character every aspect of life."[608] Once the state had moved to Egypt the caliph remained the ultimate judicial authority as the position of chief judge was usually held by an Ismaili and presumably made efforts to implement Ismaili law. However, the situation had changed by the end of the 11th CE when the chief judge did not have to follow Ismaili law. This implies that at that time executive power, that had been held in its ultimate form by the Ismaili imam-caliph, was now separated from the judiciary. To complicate matters, at this time the executive was now de facto property of the vizerate and under Badr al-Jamaili took the title "Guarantor of the Judges of Muslims and Guide of the Missionaries of the Believers" - so while in the late 11th CE judicial power was separate from the de jure executive it was not yet separate from the de facto executive! However, under later weaker viziers this can probably be said to have occurred. "After the middle of the eleventh century, even the chief judge of the Fatimid state did not have to be an Ismaili. For a time, these non-Ismaili judges were constrained to follow Ismaili law, but by the end of the eleventh century there is evidence to suggest that this was no longer the case."[609] This would be why Qutbuddin states "Judges (called qadis) from the Fatimid as well as Sunni denominations were appointed to uphold sharia law."[610] "While the Fatimids maintained their large propaganda mission (da'wa) in other parts of the Islamic world, they do not seem to have emphasized their da'wa in Egypt."[611] "Representatives of other (Sunni) madhhabs were at serveral periods heads of the Fatimid legal system, which is in itself a curious development for a dynasty committed by its very being to Ismaili Shi'ism and presumably a law based strictly on its doctrines."[612] At the end of the 11th CE the judiciary was officially taken over by the powerful viziers. Badr al-Jamaili was "a virtual dictator" although "his rank remained that of a wazir, and he was always theoretically subservient to the imam-caliph."[613] The son and successor to the famous vizier Badr al-Jamali after 1094 CE built the Palace of the Vizerate (Dar al-Wizara) which became "the official residence of the vizirs until the end of the Fatimid caliphate".[614] In 1074 CE Badr al-Jamili "took over the judiciary, appointing on his own behalf a qadi al-qudat, who, despite the title, was beholden to Badr directly."[615] "Only when al-Mu'ayyad's approaching death was obvious did Badr add the titles 'Guarantor of the Judges of Muslims and Guide of the Missionaries of the Believers ..., thereby asserting his own direct authority over both the judiciary and the da'wa."[616] Title of amir al-juyush means kafil 'guarantor' and hadi 'guide' "terms that place him above but not necessarily directly in either the judiciary or the da'wa."[617]

Legal (formal) freedoms[edit]

♠ Right to petition executive ♣ present ♥ Petitions are known from the Genizah documents. S.D. Goitein, 'Petitions to Fatimid Caliphs from the Cairo Geniza'. Jewish Quarterly Review. NS. 45 (1945-1955). pp. 30-38.
♠ Critique of executive by government ♣ absent: 909-969 CE; present: 970-1072 CE; absent: 1073-1121 CE; present: 1122-1171 CE ♥ In Tunisia the caliph directly ruled without a vizier. The court administration was run by a vizier after the move to Egypt. "The Fatimids in North Africa had appointed no wazirs and the notion of delegating that level of authority to a subordinate seemed not to have existed."[618] The court-based government also employed specialist heads for the military, treasury, chancery (domestic and foreign communications), religious and missionary activities, judiciary and an auditor (from 1011 CE).[619] The position of auditor is an interesting case as the first specialist to hold the role may have been recruited from Iraq, having worked under the 'Abbasid administration; he could have been hired for his skills to advise the Fatimid government on how to set up the office of the audit: "The post of auditor (zimam) and the office of the audit (diwan al-zimdm) are well known features of the 'Abbasid administration. In the case of the Fatimid administration, the earliest reference to diwan al-zimam is from 402/1011-1012, and the holder of the post (ndzir diwan al-zimdm) was a person of Iraqi origin with previous experience in 'Abbasid administration."[620] "Court poets in his day were held in high esteem and were often paid handsomely for their ability to, as we would say today, put the spin on delicate or uncomfortable situations, such as advising the king against a foolish course of action or bringing news of the death of a favourite slave. Poets were paid to present truth and lies in a palatable manner."[621] Critique of the executive must have become more difficult once the de facto powers of the executive had accumulated into the hands of a vizier-dictator. But the ability to critique probably returned after al-Afdal. Badr al-Jamaili was "a virtual dictator" although "his rank remained that of a wazir, and he was always theoretically subservient to the imam-caliph."[622] Badr's son al-Afdal was murdered in 1121 CE but he had "exercised the same iron-fisted control over the government as had the father."[623]
♠ Critique of executive by non-government ♣ absent ♥ No legal right to critique the imam-caliph or vizier existed.

Power distributed[edit]

♠ Sanction of executive by government ♣ absent ♥ No legal right to sanction the imam-caliph or vizier existed.
♠ Sanction of executive by non-government ♣ absent ♥ No legal right to sanction the imam-caliph or vizier existed.
♠ Constraint on executive by government ♣ absent: 909-969 CE; present: 970-1072 CE; absent: 1073-1121 CE; present: 1122-1171 CE ♥ The court based government was the legislature so there was no legislature to constrain the executive. The imam-caliph had de jure unconstrained judicial power too but from the Egyptian period onwards the non-Ismaili judiciary effectively constrained the executive from imposing an ismaili state, increasingly so towards the end of the 11th CE, most likely by withholding cooperation. "While the Fatimids maintained their large propaganda mission (da'wa) in other parts of the Islamic world, they do not seem to have emphasized their da'wa in Egypt."[624] "Judges (called qadis) from the Fatimid as well as Sunni denominations were appointed to uphold sharia law."[625] By the end of the 11th CE the chief judge did not have to follow Ismaili law. At that time the judicial constraint that already existed was most obvious as it became manifest in the religion of the highest judicial office holder. "After the middle of the eleventh century, even the chief judge of the Fatimid state did not have to be an Ismaili. For a time, these non-Ismaili judges were constrained to follow Ismaili law, but by the end of the eleventh century there is evidence to suggest that this was no longer the case."[626] By the end of the 11th CE, however, the executive was now de facto property of the vizierate and became a secular institution (the viziers could be Christian or Jewish) when under Badr al-Jamaili the vizier took the top judicial post and the title "Guarantor of the Judges of Muslims and Guide of the Missionaries of the Believers". For this reason the period of dictatorial rule of the vizierate under Badr al-Jamaili and his son al-Afdal should be considered one when the judiciary - which was at this time also the powerful executive - was not constrained by the judiciary. Badr al-Jamaili was "a virtual dictator" although "his rank remained that of a wazir, and he was always theoretically subservient to the imam-caliph."[627] Badr's son al-Afdal was murdered in 1121 CE but he had "exercised the same iron-fisted control over the government as had the father."[628] "Alexandria along with Fustat ... the only cities in Egypt with their own budget and chief judge."[629]
♠ Constraint on executive by non-government ♣ present ♥ Cooperation could be withheld by a powerful harem which had to deal with viziers. Foreign merchants had their own docks at Alexandria. "Concubines of Fatimid caliphs are known to have exercised power and influence on behalf of their sons, such as the mother of al-Mustansir (1036-94)."[630] Foreign merchants: "Still others were ships that plied the waters of the Mediterranean Sea and had sailed down the Nile to offload their goods and passengers - pilgrims, traders, artisans, Muslims, Christians, and Jews - from throughout the Mediterranean world. Some of these Mediterranean ships flew Muslim flags; others flew Byzantine, Genoese, Venetian, and Frankish flags. Clearly, political sensibilities were not to interfere with business in Fatimid Egypt."[631] Family: "The quarrel over the succession of al-Mustansir ... may have been for some of the participants primarily a matter of political intrigue and of contending interests, a struggle for power between a dictatorial minister and a prince ousted by him; but for many the question who was the rightful Imam, al-Musta'li or Nizar, must have been one of paramount religious concern."[632]
♠ Impeachment ♣ absent ♥ "A striking feature of the Fatimid dynasty is an uninterrupted father-to-son succession (also a requirement of their religious doctrine), from Mahdi to Amir - 10 imam-caliphs - for over 200 years."[633] It also is unlikely that there existed any mechanism to impeach a powerful vizier.

Customary (informal) checks on executive[edit]

♠ Informal critique of executive ♣ present ♥ Foreign merchants. "Still others were ships that plied the waters of the Mediterranean Sea and had sailed down the Nile to offload their goods and passengers - pilgrims, traders, artisans, Muslims, Christians, and Jews - from throughout the Mediterranean world. Some of these Mediterranean ships flew Muslim flags; others flew Byzantine, Genoese, Venetian, and Frankish flags. Clearly, political sensibilities were not to interfere with business in Fatimid Egypt."[634] "The Hall of Knowledge (Dar al-Ilm) was established by al-Amir in 1116 on a site just outside the southwest corner of the palace so as to lessen the scandal caused by freely voiced controversies on problems of politics and religion."[635] "According to the eleventh-century Persia da'i al-Mu'ayyad fi'l-Din al-Shirazi, the status of the al-Numans as chief da'is was secured also thanks to the support of the Fatimid royal women"[636]
♠ Informal sanction of executive ♣ inferred absent ♥ The imam-caliph or vizier could not be sanctioned.
♠ Informal constraint on executive ♣ present ♥ The harem could be considered an informal constraint on the executive. "According to the eleventh-century Persia da'i al-Mu'ayyad fi'l-Din al-Shirazi, the status of the al-Numans as chief da'is was secured also thanks to the support of the Fatimid royal women"[637] The vizier claimed to have been under pressure from 'the old women of the palace from the families of al-Hakim and al-'Aziz, [who] considered al-Nu'man as the founder of the da'wa and therefore, in their view, his sons and descendants had the greatest legal claim to the post."[638] "Concubines of Fatimid caliphs are known to have exercised power and influence on behalf of their sons, such as the mother of al-Mustansir (1036-94)."[639]
♠ Informal impeachment ♣ absent ♥ The imam-caliph or vizier could not be impeached. DH: ref?

Limits on executive power imposed by religious agents[edit]

♠ Critique of executive by religious agents ♣ present ♥ Extremist da'is preached the divinity of al-Hakim "laying the foundations of the Druze religion."[640] Mustansir crisis and the Nizari Ismailis in Persia and other eastern lands "in effect founded [an] independent Nezari da'wa."[641] "two of the most important symbols of political legitimacy in the medieval Islamic world - the authority to mint coins (sikka) and to have one's name invoked in the Friday sermon (khutba) in the congregational mosque (often built by the ruler himself."[642]
♠ Sanction of executive by religious agents ♣ absent ♥ The imam-caliph as the highest religious figure in the Fatimid Caliphate could not be sanctioned by other religious agents. "The Imam "stood, for the Ismailis, in the very centre of their religious system; it was of overriding importance; on it depended the continuity of institutional religion as well as the personal salvation of the believer. 'Whosoever dies without recognising the Imam of his time, dies a pagan's death' is one of the most often quoted maxims of Ismailism."[643]
♠ Constraint on executive by religious agents ♣ present ♥ Not Ismaili religious agents existed in both the Tunisian and Egyptian periods. "Under this dynasty these were two of the highest positions: the chief justice, termed the judge of judges (qadi al-qudat), was outranked only by the imam and the wazir. The head da'i, the da'i al-du'at, followed immediately below."[644] "After the middle of the eleventh century, even the chief judge of the Fatimid state did not have to be an Ismaili. For a time, these non-Ismaili judges were constrained to follow Ismaili law, but by the end of the eleventh century there is evidence to suggest that this was no longer the case."[645] This would be why Qutbuddin states "Judges (called qadis) from the Fatimid as well as Sunni denominations were appointed to uphold sharia law."[646] "While the Fatimids maintained their large propaganda mission (da'wa) in other parts of the Islamic world, they do not seem to have emphasized their da'wa in Egypt."[647] "Representatives of other (Sunni) madhhabs were at several periods heads of the Fatimid legal system, which is in itself a curious development for a dynasty committed by its very being to Ismaili Shi'ism and presumably a law based strictly on its doctrines."[648]
♠ Constraint on executive by religious institutions ♣ present ♥ The da'wa institution had an expansionist ideology and it was maintained abroad even as it was decreasing in influence in Fatimid regions. "Despite the similarities between the methodology and rhetoric of the Fatimids and that of the Abbasids, the Fatimid Imams belonged to the Isma'ili branch of Shi'i Islam, which was openly hostile to the Sunni Abbasid caliphate in Baghdad. In fact, the Fatimids' intent had long been to use North Africa as a staging ground for their ultimate goal of conquering Baghdad and unseating the Abbasids."[649] "While the Fatimids maintained their large propaganda mission (da'wa) in other parts of the Islamic world, they do not seem to have emphasized their da'wa in Egypt."[650] Imam-caliph needed to maintain its legitimacy as sectarian and rival doctrines that were institutionalised could switch allegiance and either deny the claim or support a rival (domestic or foreign). "The Buyid Sultan, who himself had no intention of averting the Fatimid conquest of Egypt, was happy to wage war on the Fatimids by proxy. He, with the Hamdanids of North Syria, supported the Carmathians military and financially. The situation was highly paradoxical; the Buyid Sultan, himself of declared Shi'i persuasion, used an Isma'ili group to fight another Isma'ili group. The Carmathian marched on Egypt under the black banners of the Abbasids admitting their sovereignty and legitimacy."[651] "The quarrel over the succession of al-Mustansir ... may have been for some of the participants primarily a matter of political intrigue and of contending interests, a struggle for power between a dictatorial minister and a prince ousted by him; but for many the question who was the rightful Imam, al-Musta'li or Nizar, must have been one of paramount religious concern."[652] Two separate religious institutions might be considered to be a constraint as there was a conflict of interest between Ismaili goals and non-Ismaili, which toward the late 11th CE progressively became to be resolved in the favour of non-Ismaili. "Two parallel institutions functioned ... as the guardians of religion in the public sphere: one acting as the official organ of the Ismaili imamate and its imam, the Fatimid caliph; the other dispensing justice and insuring the observance of Islamic law."[653] "On the one hand, the Fatimids came to understand that their rule over a significant population of non-Ismailis required a system of justice run by the traditional qadi of a type by then common throughout the Islamic world and, on the other, that the Ismaili community, in so far as it was separate, needed its own religious institution, in this case the da'wa. The necessity of the latter was not reduced or eliminated by the creation of the state and its Ismaili caliphate, but rather persisted as long as the Ismailis constituted a minoriy. Had they achieved clear overwhelming numerical superiority, as happened strictly among the Kutama Berbers under Abu Abd Allah prior to the victory over the Aghlabids, the situation might have been different."[654]
♠ Impeachment by religious agents ♣ absent ♥ The imam-caliph as the highest religious figure in the Fatimid Caliphate could not be impeached by other religious agents. "The Imam "stood, for the Ismailis, in the very centre of their religious system; it was of overriding importance; on it depended the continuity of institutional religion as well as the personal salvation of the believer. 'Whosoever dies without recognising the Imam of his time, dies a pagan's death' is one of the most often quoted maxims of Ismailism."[655]
♠ Subordination of the chief executive to a religious agent ♣ present ♥ The chief executive was a religious agent so in that sense the office was subordinate. "The Imam "stood, for the Ismailis, in the very centre of their religious system; it was of overriding importance; on it depended the continuity of institutional religion as well as the personal salvation of the believer. 'Whosoever dies without recognising the Imam of his time, dies a pagan's death' is one of the most often quoted maxims of Ismailism."[656]
♠ Motivation by belief in supernatural agent ♣ present ♥

Mechanisms of Power Transfer (from one head of state to the next)[edit]

♠ Customary mechanism ♣ present ♥ The Iman-caliph and his heir apparent each lived in their own palaces.[657] "A striking feature of the Fatimid dynasty is an uninterrupted father-to-son succession (also a requirement of their religious doctrine), from Mahdi to Amir - 10 imam-caliphs - for over 200 years."[658] For the position of the Imam it was "not so much the person of the claimant that weighed with his followers; they were not moved by any superior merits of Nizar as a ruler (this is, of course, obvious in the case of the infant al-Tayyib) - it was the divine right personified in the legitimate heir that counted."[659] The Fatimids claimed "biological and spiritual descent from the Prophet Muhammad through his daughter Fatimah and her husband, the first Shia imam and fourth Sunni caliph Ali ibn Abi Talib" and "the Fatimids (who also called themselves 'Alawids') challenged the rival caliphate of the Abbasids of Iraq, and asserted that they were the sole legitimate rulers of the Islamic world."[660] “al-Hakin was murdered by his owner sister, Sitt al-Mulk, not only because of the threats his unpredictable behavior posed to the dynasty, but also because of his plan to make a cousin his successor, a move that would have violated the fundamental Isam’ili principle of father-to-son succession.” [661] "
♠ Legal mechanism ♣ absent ♥ The Iman-caliph and his heir apparent each lived in their own palaces.[662] "A striking feature of the Fatimid dynasty is an uninterrupted father-to-son succession (also a requirement of their religious doctrine), from Mahdi to Amir - 10 imam-caliphs - for over 200 years."[663] For the position of the Imam it was "not so much the person of the claimant that weighed with his followers; they were not moved by any superior merits of Nizar as a ruler (this is, of course, obvious in the case of the infant al-Tayyib) - it was the divine right personified in the legitimate heir that counted."[664] The Fatimids claimed "biological and spiritual descent from the Prophet Muhammad through his daughter Fatimah and her husband, the first Shia imam and fourth Sunni caliph Ali ibn Abi Talib" and "the Fatimids (who also called themselves 'Alawids') challenged the rival caliphate of the Abbasids of Iraq, and asserted that they were the sole legitimate rulers of the Islamic world."[665] “al-Hakin was murdered by his owner sister, Sitt al-Mulk, not only because of the threats his unpredictable behavior posed to the dynasty, but also because of his plan to make a cousin his successor, a move that would have violated the fundamental Isam’ili principle of father-to-son succession.” [666] "
♠ Number of power transitions ♣ 13 ♥ 0. Abd Allah al-Mahdi (910-934 CE) 1. al-Qa'im (934-946 CE) 2. al-Mansur (946-953 CE) 3. al-Mu'izz (953-975 CE) [667] 4. Al-Aziz (975-996 CE) 5. Al-Hakim (996-1021 CE) 6. Az-Zahir (1021-1036 CE) 7. Al-Mustansir (1036-1094 CE) 8. Al-Mustali (1094-1101 CE) 9. Al-Amir (1101-1130 CE) 10. Al-Hafiz (1130-1149 CE) 11. Az-Zahir (1140-1154 CE) 12. Al-Faiz (!154-1160 CE) 13. Al-Azid (1160-1171 CE)
♠ Number of contested transitions ♣ ♥ 1094 CE “year of the death of caliphs and commanders” long-lived Fatimid caliph al-Mustansir (natural causes?) and his vizier Badr al-Jamali (de facto ruler of state).[668] During the Mustansir Crisis the state had 27 difference viziers between 1060-1066 CE.[669] Caliph al-Amir was murdered and power seized by his cousin 'Abd al-Majid.[670] “al-Hakin was murdered by his owner sister, Sitt al-Mulk, not only because of the threats his unpredictable behavior posed to the dynasty, but also because of his plan to make a cousin his successor, a move that would have violated the fundamental Isam’ili principle of father-to-son succession.” [671] "A striking feature of the Fatimid dynasty is an uninterrupted father-to-son succession (also a requirement of their religious doctrine), from Mahdi to Amir - 10 imam-caliphs - for over 200 years."[672] The stability of succession likely also due to fact viziers, not the Iman-caliph, for a long time held the de facto power. In 1084 CE vizier Badr al-Jamali's son al-Awhad "led a group of soldiers and Bedouins in revolt against his father".[673] However, a change of viziers might have had consequences on the succession to Iman-caliph. Badr's son al-Afdal was murdered in 1121 CE but he had "exercised the same iron-fisted control over the government as had the father."[674]
♠ Number of successful overturns ♣ ♥

Central Bureaucracy[edit]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ present ♥

The polity began in Tunisia under the rule of the Iman-caliph. The Iman-caliph "ruled directly in the north Africa period and was aided by a vizier in the Egyptian phase."[675] Initially "Among the ranks of the Fatimid movement - a clandestine organization whose greatest achievement was to win to its cause a peripheral social group, the Berbers of Kutama - the know-how needed for running a state was not to be found."[676] In terms of governance this suggests "The degree of continuity between Aghlabid and early Fatimid Tunisia must have been very marked."[677] In Tunisia, the Fatimids used slave eunuchs to command army and naval forces. Like typical Islamic regimes they founded cities as "seats for their courts and as administrative and military centres for their regimes."[678] "From the inception of Fatimid rule in Tunisia, black military slaves (al-sudan) and white military slaves (Rum) of the Aghlabids were employed in the Fatimid army."[679] The impressive cities of Mahdiyya and Mansuriyya were built.[680] Mansuriyya "had water brought from the distant spring of 'Ayn Ayyub through an aqueduct modeled on the Roman system at Carthage."[681]

"The Fatimid caliph was not just an imperial ruler. He was also the Ismaili imam, the spiritual leader of the believers".[682] "The ruler himself sat on a gold throne protected by a curtain in a fashion inspired by the Abbasids and resembling Byzantine enthronement. He was entitled to a special crown, sword, scepter, parasol, weapons, and other implements that were signs of his sovereignty. An elaborate protocol regulated eating, drinking, and dressing. One of the prerogatives of the ruler was deferential behavior in his presence - silence, dismounting, and kissing the ground." [683] "A hierarchical corps of eunuchs controlled the harem and the personal life of the caliph."[684] Nasir-i Khusraw (c1048 CE) records that 30,000 people were employed by the court[685] and that "fourteen camel loads of snow were delivered each day to the sultan's pantries." [686]

Late 11th century viziers began to call themselves malik (prince).[687]"The Armenian wazir, Badr al-Jamali, is certainly one of the most famous of these and was the de facto ruler of the Fatimid state (1074-94) as wazir to the Fatimid caliph, al-Mustansir (r. 1036-94)." [688] From vizier Badr al-Jamali "Henceforth, with minor exceptions, real power in the Fatimid state remained in the hands of viziers who possessed military bases of power and acted independently, while the caliphs remained the nominal heads of state and as Ismaili imams also functioned as supreme leaders of the ismalil da'wa or religious organization. A distinguishing feature of the Fatimid vizierate during its final century is that several viziers were Christians, notably Armenians."[689] Badr al-Jamali built encircling wall around Qahira and reported by Maqrizi the first time allowed people to settle in the caliphal city.[690] The son and successor to the famous vizier Badr al-Jamali after 1094 CE built the Palace of the Vizerate (Dar al-Wizara) which became "the official residence of the vizirs until the end of the Fatimid caliphate".[691]

Heads of administration (e.g. military, treasury, religious, missionary, and judiciary).[692] "Poets were employed by the state to fill the role of modern-day public relations agents."[693] Chancery sent and received messages within the Empire and overseas.[694] "The post of auditor (zimam) and the office of the audit (diwan al-zimdm) are well known features of the 'Abbasid administration. In the case of the Fatimid administration, the earliest reference to diwan al-zimam is from 402/1011-1012, and the holder of the post (ndzir diwan al-zimdm) was a person of Iraqi origin with previous experience in 'Abbasid administration."[695] "Al-Zahir is credited with establishing the state's armory (khizdna al-bunud), which was an arsenal and a workshop employing 3,000 craftsmen for producing arms."[696] Officials were responsible for standardized measures (iyar) and there was an overseer of the mint.[697]

"Under al-Mu'zz's son and successor, al-'Aziz (d. 386/996), court life flourished and the Fatimid dynasty (dawla) reached its political, territorial and economic zenith."[698] The da'wa was a "unique institution of religiopolitical proselytizing and education".[699] "the da'wa organisation ... evolved into a core institution of the Fatimid regime. By al-'Aziz's time, the da'is came to be formally organised into a complex hierarchical structure headed by a chief da'i. From Cairo, the imam-caliph seconded da'is to covertly set up new 'cells' in regions outside Fatimid control, as well as to overtly run the existing 'cells' scattered around Fatimid domains."[700] "Under this dynasty these were two of the highest positions: the chief justice, termed the judge of judges (qadi al-qudat), was outranked only by the imam and the wazir. The head da'i, the da'i al-du'at, followed immediately below."[701] The da'wa and its top administrator was, according to Al-Maqrizi, a khitta or "an office or institution of government".[702]

♠ Prestige of state-level bureaucracy ♣ higher ♥ Lower: 909-969 CE; higher: 970-1171 CE

"The one decree that lacks a name of the appointee carefully explains that it is God and God alone who requires no wazir. But humans do. If any one, they say, could have not needed a wazir, it would have been Moses. Nonetheless, it was he who asked God to appoint him a wazir - Aaron, his brother (per Qur'anic report). Muhammad's wazir ..."[703]

"The Fatimids in North Africa had appointed no wazirs and the notion of delegating that level of authority to a subordinate seemed not to have existed."[704]

Decree dated 1028 CE "states quite clearly that wazirs are not and do not diminish the status of the caliph-imam."[705]

"Court poets in his day were held in high esteem and were often paid handsomely for their ability to, as we would say today, put the spin on delicate or uncomfortable situations, such as advising the king against a foolish course of action or bringing news of the death of a favourite slave. Poets were paid to present truth and lies in a palatable manner."[706]

Source of Support of state-level officials

♠ State salary ♣ present ♥ "Religious scholars, jurists, scientists and librarians worked at the Dar al-'Ilm, drawing salaries from the Fatimid treasury or that institution's endowment set up by al-Hakim himself."[731] End of 10th CE "salaries were paid eight times a year, but by the middle of the eleventh century, payment was monthly. In addition to their monthly salaries, soldiers in the capital received special bonuses, and troops generally received a special payment before a campaign or when a new caliph ascended the throne. The iqta system, which increasingly replaced cash payments to the military, required a very substantial bureaucracy to record and administer the land grants, many of which were in the provinces."[732] In mid-11th CE an office "registered the names of both civil and military employees who received cash salaries and distributed payments"[733]
♠ Governed population ♣ present ♥ “Qadis, administrative personnel, and members of the royal family received grants of iqta’ in lieu of their salaries of part of their remuneration….during al-Hakim’s reign the circle of those receiving iqta’ was enlarged to include soldiers and ‘abid al-shira [black slaves].” [734] "1021 to 1073, saw a breakdown of political order, warfare between military factions, and the division of the country into land tax allotments controlled by leading military officers".[735]

Pathologies

♠ Corruption ♣ ♥ Nasir Khusraw observed that "the chief justice receives a monthly stipend of 2,000 dinars, and a proportionate amount is paid to every judge down the ranks."[738] Nasir Khusraw opinion was that the salaried judges were less likely to be bought and sold[739] but we know was their relatively high pay was not completely effective at eliminating corruption: "we have several cases of Fatimid judges who fell victim to their own venality with respect to the funds of orphans."[740]
♠ Bribery ♣ ♥ Nasir Khusraw observed that "the chief justice receives a monthly stipend of 2,000 dinars, and a proportionate amount is paid to every judge down the ranks."[741] Nasir Khusraw opinion was that the salaried judges were less likely to be bought and sold[742] but we know was their relatively high pay was not completely effective at eliminating corruption: "we have several cases of Fatimid judges who fell victim to their own venality with respect to the funds of orphans."[743]
♠ Abuse of power ♣ ♥ Nasir Khusraw observed that "the chief justice receives a monthly stipend of 2,000 dinars, and a proportionate amount is paid to every judge down the ranks."[744] Nasir Khusraw opinion was that the salaried judges were less likely to be bought and sold[745] but we know was their relatively high pay was not completely effective at eliminating corruption: "we have several cases of Fatimid judges who fell victim to their own venality with respect to the funds of orphans."[746]

Local-level officials (provincial, regional, civic administration)[edit]

♠ Number of regional administrative units ♣ ♥

Al-Muqaddasi (10th CE) divided Egypt into seven districts. 1. Settled Mediterranean coast if Sinai 2,3,4. Nile delta region 5. South of delta (capital Fustat) 6. the Sa'id or Upper Egypt (capital Aswan) 7. Desert and oases. [759]

"Alexandria along with Fustat ... the only cities in Egypt with their own budget and chief judge."[760]


♠ Local-level administrators ♣ present ♥ Were there any provinces in Egypt e.g. Upper Egypt? How were provinces in Egypt commanded? By military governor? (Amir?).

Local administration in Fustat

"The town of Fustat was governed by a wali (governor) who was effectively chief of police. A muhtasib supervised the activities of shopkeepers and artisans and saw to the observance of religious law."[761]
"Trash was carted away for a fee that varied according to the size of the building and the frequency of collection."[762]
"The digging, clearing , and maintenance of underground channels was also subject to official supervision."[763]
"Excavations of houses in Fustat, particularly those uncovered by Scanlon, do in fact confirm that a complex and highly developed sewer system was in place."[764]
In Fustat the muhtasib "supervised the activities of shopkeepers and artisans and saw to the observance of religious law." [765]
"upkeep costs of the streets themselves were borne by the streets' residents."[766] The masons of the road repairs were perhaps supervised by authorities.[767]
In Fustat residential zones appeared to be closed neighbourhoods, or quarters. "The Geniza documents mention neighborhood supervisors (sahib al-rub and hami al-hara), officials who apparently exercised authority over the quarter and saw to its security, particularly at night. The sentinels who walked the nightly rounds were paid for by a monthly tax (the guard duty, or hirasa), which figures in the accounts of the Jewish community. The sahib al-rub also directed communal activities: it was he who assembled the people of his quarter to greet the sovereign on his triumphal entry into the city."[768] Porter-guards "opened and closed the alleys through which one entered quarters, some of which had gates or posterns."[769]

Jewish community

Jewish community "was governed by a ra'is al-Yahud, who became known as the najid (the title would appear in Egypt around 1065), and owned sufficient property to finance charitable foundations and aid the needy."[770]

Missionary operations (da'wa) had a local management structure

"In theory, it was the imam-caliph who would ultimately determine the rank each missionary would be allocated on the basis of the level of his esoteric spiritual attainment, scholarly credentials and seniority, as well as political and managerial skills. ... In practice, and especially in outposts away from the heart of the dawla, the management of the distribution of ranks was deputised to the local chief da'i, who was de facto in charge of particular areas of operation or 'islands' (jaza'ir)."[771]

Authority over local-level administrators

♠ Direct ♣ absent ♥ "Alexandria along with Fustat ... the only cities in Egypt with their own budget and chief judge."[772]
♠ Indirect ♣ present ♥ "The town of Fustat was governed by a wali (governor) who was effectively chief of police. A muhtasib supervised the activities of shopkeepers and artisans and saw to the observance of religious law."[773] "Alexandria along with Fustat ... the only cities in Egypt with their own budget and chief judge."[774]
♠ Diffused ♣ present ♥ Vassal Isma'ili state in Multan overthrown by Gaznavids 1005-1006 CE[775] would have been virtually independent. Abu Yaqub Sejestani (d c.971 CE), da'i of Khorasan, "now endorsed the imamate of the Fatimids and propagated their cause in Knorasan, Sistan, and Makran, where numerous Ismailis rallied to the side of the Fatimid da'wa. The Fatimid da'is also succeeded around 347/958 in establishing a Fatimid vassal state centered in Moltan, in northern India, where the kotba was now read in the name of the Fatimid caliphs, instead of their Abbasid rivals.".[776] According to Nasir Khusraw princes were very highly paid and had few obligations: "No aristocrat receives less than five hundred dinars, some drawing stipends of up to two thousand dinars. The only function they have to perform is to make a salaam to the grand vizier, when he sits in state, and then withdraw to their places (S, 50)."[777] "The practice of paying princes 'from far away' to publicly make obeisance during official ceremonies allowed the Fatimids to exercise control over them and demonstrate at home the far-flung political reach of their empire."[778]

♠ Mixed ♣ present ♥ Jewish community "was governed by a ra'is al-Yahud, who became known as the najid (the title would appear in Egypt around 1065), and owned sufficient property to finance charitable foundations and aid the needy."[779] Government paid for "all expenses incurred by mosques (at least from Syria to Tunisia, an area under direct Fatimid control and the extent of Nasir's record of this issue). The royal treasury paid for all the carpets and rugs, all the salaries for those tending the mosques such as watchmen, cleaners and..."[780]

Source of Support of local-level officials

♠ State salary ♣ present ♥ According to Nasir Khusraw princes were very highly paid and had few obligations: "No aristocrat receives less than five hundred dinars, some drawing stipends of up to two thousand dinars. The only function they have to perform is to make a salaam to the grand vizier, when he sits in state, and then withdraw to their places (S, 50)."[781] "The practice of paying princes 'from far away' to publicly make obeisance during official ceremonies allowed the Fatimids to exercise control over them and demonstrate at home the far-flung political reach of their empire."[782]

♠ None ♣ present ♥ Da'is in foreign lands worked undercover. Most common job of da'is was merchant to sell goods, including to women. This could be used as an excuse to approach and speak to potential converts to "test the ground".[783]

Legal System[edit]

♠ Multiple legal systems ♣ present ♥

Ismaili

Al-Mu'izz (953-975 CE) "was responsible for the promulgation and implementation of a distinctive Isma'ili law, mainly formulated by al-Qadi al-Nu'man, which aimed at imprinting with an Isma'ili character every aspect of life."[786]

Sunni Islam and Ismaili Islam

"Judges (called qadis) from the Fatimid as well as Sunni denominations were appointed to uphold sharia law."[787] "Representatives of other (Sunni) madhhabs were at serveral periods heads of the Fatimid legal system, which is in itself a curious development for a dynasty committed by its very being to Ismaili Shi'ism and presumably a law based strictly on its doctrines."[788]
"On the one hand, the Fatimids came to understand that their rule over a significant population of non-Ismailis required a system of justice run by the traditional qadi of a type by then common throughout the Islamic world and, on the other, that the Ismaili community, in so far as it was separate, needed its own religious institution, in this case the da'wa. The necessity of the latter was not reduced or eliminated by the creation of the state and its Ismaili caliphate, but rather persisted as long as the Ismailis constituted a minoriy. Had they achieved clear overwhelming numerical superiority, as happened strictly among the Kutama Berbers under Abu Abd Allah prior to the victory over the Aghlabids, the situation might have been different."[789]
"Judges hold authority over Muslims, all Muslims, the broader and more inclusive category, while da'is administer to the needs of the true believers, a much smaller but more select group within the larger body of Islam. Strictly from the viewpoint of a believer, the chief da'i is more important than the chief qadi; quite possibly in this respect he holds higher authority, even with regard to questions involving the application of law. But, for the common citizen of the Fatimid empire, non-Muslims and especially non-Ismaili Muslims, the qadi remained, in part because of the greater numbers of those who required what he provided, more important. Most inhabitants of the realm never accepted da'wa and they had no dealings with the da'i."[790]

Judaism

From the Genizah documents: "One of the kidnapped women insisted, however, that she was “a Jew in her hometown.” To emphasize this point she added, "I have not had any dealings with Muslims.” The qadi therefore decided that a Jewish court should handle the case, since the Jewish authorities could better confirm her identity."[791]

♠ Courts are supported by the state ♣ present ♥

Al-Mu'izz (953-975 CE) "was responsible for the promulgation and implementation of a distinctive Isma'ili law, mainly formulated by al-Qadi al-Nu'man, which aimed at imprinting with an Isma'ili character every aspect of life."[792]

"Two parallel institutions functioned ... as the guardians of religion in the public sphere: one acting as the official organ of the Ismaili imamate and its imam, the Fatimid caliph; the other dispensing justice and insuring the observance of Islamic law."[793]

♠ Criminal Prosecution ♣ ♥

From the Genizah documents: A dispute concerning an a contested accusation that a Jewish merchant had abandoned a slave in the Red Sea port of Aydhab was held before the governor. The plaintiff was a slave-agent.[794] Were disputes between two Muslims also held before a governor. Genizah documents refer to "court records".[795] "One of the kidnapped women insisted, however, that she was “a Jew in her hometown.” To emphasize this point she added, "I have not had any dealings with Muslims.” The qadi therefore decided that a Jewish court should handle the case, since the Jewish authorities could better confirm her identity."[796]

Inheritance System

♠ partible inheritance ♣ absent ♥ No inheritance in the iqta' system "as early as 359/970 the general Jawhar ordered the Maliki qadi of the former Ikhshidid capital Fustat, Abu Tahir, to apply Fatimid law in cases of divorce and inheritance, an injunction which was further enforced following the arrival of al-Mu'izz in Egypt three years later."[898]
♠ patrilineal inheritance ♣ absent ♥ No inheritance in the iqta' system "as early as 359/970 the general Jawhar ordered the Maliki qadi of the former Ikhshidid capital Fustat, Abu Tahir, to apply Fatimid law in cases of divorce and inheritance, an injunction which was further enforced following the arrival of al-Mu'izz in Egypt three years later."[899]
♠ matrilineal inheritance ♣ absent ♥ No inheritance in the iqta' system "Following his enthronement in Raqqah, al-Mahdi instructed the local jurists to give legal opinions on the basis of Shi'i principles rooted in the teachings of Ja'far al-Sadiq. The gradual reform of the mainly Maliki judicial system in Ifriqiya signalled a major shift in the purpose of the da'wa, from being essentially an ideological and revolutionary agency to one also responsible for the implementation and administration of Shi'i-inspired laws Beside the domain of ritual, these new laws addressed family law, thus affecting - at least in theory - the legal status of women. For example, in keeping with Ja'farite inheritance law, women found themselves being granted extended inheritance rights on the basis of the legal precedent of Fatima's claim to the Fadak orchard."[900] "as early as 359/970 the general Jawhar ordered the Maliki qadi of the former Ikhshidid capital Fustat, Abu Tahir, to apply Fatimid law in cases of divorce and inheritance, an injunction which was further enforced following the arrival of al-Mu'izz in Egypt three years later."[901]

Legal rights are different on the basis of:

♠ Gender ♣ present ♥ "Following his enthronement in Raqqah, al-Mahdi instructed the local jurists to give legal opinions on the basis of Shi'i principles rooted in the teachings of Ja'far al-Sadiq. The gradual reform of the mainly Maliki judicial system in Ifriqiya signalled a major shift in the purpose of the da'wa, from being essentially an ideological and revolutionary agency to one also responsible for the implementation and administration of Shi'i-inspired laws Beside the domain of ritual, these new laws addressed family law, thus affecting - at least in theory - the legal status of women. For example, in keeping with Ja'farite inheritance law, women found themselves being granted extended inheritance rights on the basis of the legal precedent of Fatima's claim to the Fadak orchard."[909]
♠ Social class ♣ present ♥ "The writ of manumission is another more common document type that survived in the Genizah. Such writs had two kinds of value: for freed slaves, they provided proof of free status; they also provided proof, when courts requested it, of the identities and marriageable status of their offspring."[910]
♠ Ethnicity or race ♣ inferred present ♥ “In a state that had been marked by its tolerance of Jews, Christians and Sunnis, al-Hakim introduced numerous repressive measures against these groups.” [911]

The delivery of sessions for the instruction of women (as well as men) was indeed one of the duties that a da'i was required to commit himself to, when taking his oath of allegiance."[915]

Discrimination on the basis of social class (include de facto limitations even when there are no explicit legal restrictions)

♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to people from all social classes ♣ inferred absent ♥ da'is were "appointed only by the imam's permission, enjoyed a high degree of autonomy in the regions under their jurisdiction. As a result, only persons of high educational qualifications combined with the required intellectual and moral attributes, and organizational abilities, could aspire to that position."[916] "Evidence from the Maghrib and from much later in Egypt suggest that various members of the same family frequently held posts in the judiciary either from one generation to the next or simultaneously."[917] Administration staffed by non-Muslim slaves (or Muslim converts): "The long domination of Fatimid administration by Ibn Killis, a converted Iraqi Jew, first as minister of finances and then as vizir from 977 to 991, and the brilliant military career of Fadl b. Salih, a converted Christian, from 979 to 1009, were part and parcel of the dominance of Christians and Jews within the central administration."[918]
♠ Promotion in administrative positions is open to people from all social classes ♣ inferred absent ♥ "In theory, it was the imam-caliph who would ultimately determine the rank each missionary would be allocated on the basis of the level of his esoteric spiritual attainment, scholarly credentials and seniority, as well as political and managerial skills. ... In practice, and especially in outposts away from the heart of the dawla, the management of the distribution of ranks was deputised to the local chief da'i, who was de facto in charge of particular areas of operation or 'islands' (jaza'ir)."[919] da'is were "appointed only by the imam's permission, enjoyed a high degree of autonomy in the regions under their jurisdiction. Social Mobility[edit]

Status[edit]

Elite status

♠ elite status is hereditary ♣ present ♥ The Fatimid vizier al-Afdal was "the son and successor to the vizir Badr al-Jamali." [935] For the position of the Imam it was "not so much the person of the claimant that weighed with his followers; they were not moved by any superior merits of Nizar as a ruler (this is, of course, obvious in the case of the infant al-Tayyib) - it was the divine right personified in the legitimate heir that counted."[936] "Evidence from the Maghrib and from much later in Egypt suggest that various members of the same family frequently held posts in the judiciary either from one generation to the next or simultaneously."[937]
♠ elite status is selected ♣ present ♥ da'is were "appointed only by the imam's permission, enjoyed a high degree of autonomy in the regions under their jurisdiction. As a result, only persons of high educational qualifications combined with the required intellectual and moral attributes, and organizational abilities, could aspire to that position."[938] "In theory, it was the imam-caliph who would ultimately determine the rank each missionary would be allocated on the basis of the level of his esoteric spiritual attainment, scholarly credentials and seniority, as well as political and managerial skills. ... In practice, and especially in outposts away from the heart of the dawla, the management of the distribution of ranks was deputised to the local chief da'i, who was de facto in charge of particular areas of operation or 'islands' (jaza'ir)."[939] Highest offices of the vizierate and army "included several Sunni Muslims, and also Jews and (Coptic and Melkite) Christians, indicating a largely tolerant regime."[940]

"In the Mediterranean of the tenth to the twelfth centuries, men enjoyed an extraordinary degree of freedom of movement. The existence of political boundaries, even hostilities between governments, rarely interfered with the movement of persons or goods around the Mediterranean."[945]

"Christians and Jews continued to be employed in government service during the Fatimid (969-1171) and Ayyubid (1171-1250) periods ... According to S. D. Goitein, it was the 'unattractiveness of government service which made it available to non-Muslims at a time when plenty of other outlets for economic activity were open to Muslims. This explains why [working with hot melted metals in] the mints in most Muslim states remained largely a domain of the Jews even in later periods.'"[949] It was in the thirteen century when Islamic economies "became increasingly monopolized by the state, Muslims began to seek government posts at the expense of Christians and Jews."[950]


Slavery[edit]

♠ Proportion of population enslaved ♣ ♥ "the majority of slaves in the Islamic empire at any given time served primarily as domestic servants and slave soldiers."[951]

Slaves came as baqt gifts from Nubia which was part of a reciprocal exchange process not a tribute payment that saw wheat, cereals and textiles going the other way.[952]

Household slavery known for Egyptian Jewish community.[953]

"My analysis of these sources demonstrates that over the course of the twelfth century, Egyptians turned increasingly southward toward sub-Saharan Africa and eastward toward the Indian Ocean for slave imports."[954]

"Domestic slaves were intimately embedded in household life, where free women used them to protect their social status and extend their own practical kin networks."

Slaves usually purchased.[955]

"female slaves are found far more commonly in the Genizah than their male counterparts. The predominance of female slaves in the historical record probably reflects a historical reality in which domestic work was recognized as the domain of women - slave and free. The other type of slave frequently found in Genizah records is the ghulam ... the male slave-agent (factotum) of a merchant or other official."[956]

Genizah documents attest to the fact kidnapping of free people by raiders who sold them on to slave traders was a source of slaves.[957]

Prisoners of war were a source of slaves.[958]

"severe poverty, material distress, and famine could compel families to sell their children. As al-Maqrizi (1364-1442) notes in his history of Ayyubid Egypt, farmers and parents in Upper Egypt (al-Said) were forced to reduce their children to slavery."[959]

"Genizah documents that document the jins of a slave, Nubians comprise 39% of the total slave population. The other groups are "black" (13%), Indian (13%), muwalladah (born into slavery or mixed race, 13%), Persian (6%), and Byzantine (6%). There are also single slaves identified as Frankish, North African (maghribiyyah), and Abyssinian, whom I have identified as "other" in order to simplify this particular classificatory scheme (10%)."[960]

"From the tenth century onward, Christian slaves in general, and slaves from the Byzantine and Venetian slave trades more specifically, became increasingly off-limits for slave traders carrying their human chattel to Muslim controlled territories. In contrast, Michael McCormick has documented a robust ninth-century slave trade in European slaves to the Islamic world."[961]

"Medieval chronicles and other narrative accounts provide suggestive details about the slave trade in the greater Mediterranean and Near East between the ninth and thirteenth centuries. More than that, they provide a valuable framework for constructing a larger, inter-regional view of the slave trade that we would otherwise be unable to recreate from documentary sources alone. Those sources suggest that there were four main methods of enslavement: kidnapping, raiding, piracy, and material distress. States forcibly transported slaves as part of the diplomatic exchange of prestige gifts while opportunistic slave dealers also funneled slaves from Egypt’s southern frontier."[962]

General reference slavery Islamic lands: "Slave traders also brought human chattel from diverse and distant locations to the urban centers of the Islamic world. Slaves from western and central Europe were funneled along with Slavic captives across the Mediterranean. To the east, peoples from the Caucasus and Central Asia were brought overland to Baghdad and later to Cairo. Abyssinian, Nubian and East African peoples also composed a significant part of the slave-supply that streamed into the Near East." [963]

"The common use of domestic slaves reflects the relative wealth and urban character of the Islamic imperium. The families of merchants, judges, scholars and, at times, even artisans were able to purchase slaves to help with the raising of children and day-to-day household chores. Slave women were frequently used as child-bearing concubines by Muslim men. But according to Islamic law, children born to a Muslim master and a female slave were considered a free born Muslims. Mothers of such children gained the status of umm al-walad, literally “mother of the child.” Islamic law stipulated that an umm al-walad could not be sold after she bore her master’s child. Furthermore, she gained her own freedom upon the death of her master. While Islamic law held that a child born to two slave parents was also a slave, the rate of this natural increase was insufficient to ensure the replenishment of the slave population."[964]

While Islamic law still recognized captives as While Islamic law still recognized captives as eligible for slavery provided that they were unbelievers captured in Jihad, the rapid rate of conquest and expansion throughout North Africa and the Middle East gradually slowed after the eighth century. This meant that the numbers of slaves entering the empire as prisoners-of-war declined. Additionally, enslavement of populations within Muslim controlled territories ceased to be a reliable source for recruitment, since conversion to Islam and “the acceptance of dhimmi [protected] status by increasing numbers of non-Muslims” reduced the available pool of eligible slaves."[965]

"African slaves were prevalent in the lands of the Middle East since pre-Islamic times. Bilal, the first muezzin, was a manumitted black slave of the Prophet".[966]

"Concubines of Fatimid caliphs are known to have exercised power and influence on behalf of their sons, such as the mother of al-Mustansir (1036-94)."[967]

"From the inception of Fatimid rule in Tunisia, black military slaves (al-sudan) and white military slaves (Rum) of the Aghlabids were employed in the Fatimid army."[968]

"The existence of a slave component in the Fatimid army did not jeopardise the numerical superiority or the political predominance of the Kutama Berbers."[969]

"As for the women the da'i should employ in his house, beside his wives, they should either be relatives whom he would commit to marry according to shari'a rulings or be his slaves' wives."[970]

"The writ of manumission is another more common document type that survived in the Genizah. Such writs had two kinds of value: for freed slaves, they provided proof of free status; they also provided proof, when courts requested it, of the identities and marriageable status of their offspring."[973]
